<text>There are better beaches nearby for just relaxing in the sand or checking out beautiful coastal cliffs and landscapes. Where this beach shines is the tidepools. There are lots of quality tidepools to admire over a long stretch of distance filled with black and brown tegula snails, blue band hermit crabs, rough limpets, striped shore crabs, gooseneck barnacles and little brown barnacles, aggregating and suburst anemones, rockweed, turkish washcloth, and many other types of life. < review>
This is our Rosie! He (yes, Rosie is a he..long story) was a "special needs" ferret from the start, with a snaggle tooth and somewhat short, squat and malformed body. When we got him, we actually picked out and paid for his brother, and then had the conversation that the breeders would probably kill this goofy-looking little runt, so we bought him too!
We took them to Dr. Greek, and during Rosie's 2nd exam, Dr Greek realized he had a large tumor on his kidney. Dr. Greek basically told us that we should get our money back, that the breeders were probably garbage, but we knew what would happen to Rosie if we did that, so we've spent the last 4+ years giving this sweet little baby the best life we could, knowing his life would most likely be short.
My husband and I went on vacation, and while we were out of the country, Rosie took a turn for the worse. Our young adult son was home trying to deal with this, and was very distraught. He'd called a local 24 hour vet, but was refused. We were stuck on a layover, it was early in the morning, so I sent a message through Dr Greek's website to please contact my son to bring Rosie in, as he was clearly dying and in pain.
They contacted my son before they were officially open for business and my son was able to bring Rosie in, and they helped him pass quickly and humanely.
We've been bringing our ferrets to Dr Greek for many years, and have always known the staff to be AMAZING, but this horrible situation was made so much easier because they flat-out care about animals and their people.<//review>

We left my rabbit for her surgery and were told to come back later to pick her up. When we did, they gave me painkillers to give her but no antibiotics or supplemental food. (Rabbits can be prone to not eating after their surgery so it is recommended to give them supplemental food. If they do not eat or poop for a day, they will likely die. Supplemental food by the way, is not prescription and easy to buy but only in the very few places that sell them. They wouldn't tell me where else to get some.) I insisted on them giving me supplemental food but they said no. Call them the next afternoon if she still doesn't eat. I asked if she had eaten at all in their care and they said no. I called the office the next morning and told them that she still wasn't eating and could I please pick up some supplemental food? They told me no again. It hadn't been 24 hours yet, so therefore her life was not in danger yet. (Their life can be in danger starting from 6 hours of not eating or pooping). They told me they would talk to the doctor and call me back.
24 hours after the surgery, still nothing. I brought my rabbit to the emergency clinic that night because by then, still nothing. She was lethargic and not eating. Turns out, she had a fever from an infection and a stomach problem from not eating. We eventually took her to her good vet and they gave us medications and supplemental food for her. It took her two weeks to recover when it should have taken 48 hours for her to be that much better.
2 days after my call for help? Dr. Greek's office called me to ask me how she was doing.
By then, she could have died.
